State v. Dahl


SC S41948


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


323 Ore. 199; 915 P.2d 979; 1996 Ore. LEXIS 43


January 5, 1996, Argued and submitted  
May 9, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] DC Z157060; CA A83202. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from Multnomah County District Court, Aaron Brown, Judge. 132 Ore. App. 232, 888 P.2d 606 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the district court are reversed. This case is remanded to the district court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.010.


OVERVIEW: An anonymous caller informed the police that defendant was waving a gun on the front porch of a specified address. The police observed defendant make a wild swerve over the left side of the street, leave his car, and enter his house. A police dispatcher called defendant, with officers stationed outside of the house, and ordered defendant to come out with his hands up. Defendant came out of his house, and field sobriety tests showed that he was intoxicated. The state admitted that it did not have probable cause to arrest defendant when he was in the house. Defendant was convicted of violating § 813.010, and the appellate court affirmed the conviction. The court reversed and held that when defendant was ordered to come out of the house with his hands up, he was seized by the police officers within the meaning of Or. Const. art. I, § 9. Because there was no probable cause for defendant's warrantless seizure, and there were no exigent circumstances, the fruits of the seizure, including the sobriety test and defendant's admission that he had been driving, were erroneously admitted into evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court, which affirmed the district court's conviction of defendant for driving under the influence of intoxicants. The case was remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

 

40 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


McIntire v. Forbes


S42561


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 426; 909 P.2d 846; 1996 Ore. LEXIS 4


September 26, 1995, Argued and submitted  
January 19, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition challenging the constitutionality of sections 1 to 17 of Senate Bill 1156 (1995 Special Session).



DISPOSITION: Sections 1 to 17 of SB 1156 are declared void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, residents and taxpayers, challenged the constitutionality of the light rail funding provisions of S.B. 1156, Spec. Sess. (Or. 1995). Petitioners claimed that the bill violated the "one-subject" requirement of Or. Const. art. IV, § 20. Respondents, Director of the Oregon Department of Transportation, the State Treasurer, and intervenor entity with light rail responsibilities, claimed that petitioners lacked standing.


OVERVIEW: For a number of reasons, petitioner residents and taxpayers challenged the constitutionality of the light rail funding provisions of S.B. 1156, Spec. Sess. (Or. 1995) (Act). Respondents, Director of the Oregon Department of Transportation and the State Treasurer, and intervenor entity with light rail responsibilities claimed that petitioners did not have standing and the court did not have jurisdiction to a challenge made under Or. Const. art. IV, § 20, which required that the Act contain only one subject. The court concluded that it had jurisdiction because the legislature intended that challenges to the Act would be resolved promptly and petitioners had standing as interested persons. Examining the body of the Act, the court could not identify a unifying principle that logically connected all its provisions, which included diverse subjects such as providing funding for light rail, regulating confined animal feeding, and adopting new timber harvesting rules. The Act's title failed to express a unifying principle because it did little more than define the universe with respect to which the legislature was empowered to act. The court held that §§ 1-17 of S.B. 1156 were void.


OUTCOME: The court held that petitioner residents and taxpayers were "interested persons" who had standing to challenge the constitutionality of a senate bill. Certain sections of the bill were void because they embraced more than one subject, including funding for light rail, new timber harvesting rules, and the protection of salmon from cormorants, and thereby violated the Oregon Constitution's "one-subject" requirement.

 

41 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Greist v. Phillips


SC S41542


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 281; 906 P.2d 789; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 125


January 10, 1995, Argued and submitted  
November 24, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 90-1879-L-1; CA A76287. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Jackson County Circuit Court, L.A. Merryman, Judge. 128 Ore. App. 390, 875 P.2d 1199 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a wrongful death action filed by plaintiff decedent's estate, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.020, against defendants, the driver and the owner of a commercial truck, the Oregon Court of Appeals reversed the estate's noneconomic damage award, which was reduced by the trial court to the statutory cap found at Or. Rev. Stat. § 18.560, on the ground that the cap violated Or. Const. art. VII, § 3 as amended. Defendants appealed the reversal.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was a 10-month old child who was killed in a car/truck crash. The beneficiaries of the decedent's estate were his family members. The jury awarded the estate $ 100,000 in economic damages and $ 1.5 Million in noneconomic damages. The trial court reduced the non-economic award to the § 18.560 statutory cap of $ 500,000. The lower appellate court reduced the reduction after holding that the cap violated Or. Const. art. VII, § 3 as amended. The court reversed the lower appellate court's decision. The court held that (1) the trial court did not err in interpreting § 30.020, the wrongful death statute, when it applied a single $ 500,000 limit on noneconomic damages delineated in Or. Rev. Stat. § 18.560(1), and (2) the $ 500,000 noneconomic damages cap was constitutional. The court observed that because an action for wrongful death was purely statutory, the Legislature had the right to define the terms and limits of the relief afforded by the action. The damage cap statute, § 30.020 did not violate due process or equal protection principles because it did not create a separate an unequal class by its application, and because there was a rational basis for its enactment.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower appellate court, which held that the noneconomic damages cap statute was unconstitutional, was reversed. In all other respects, it was affirmed.

Meltebeke v. Bureau of Labor & Indus.


SC S40567


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 132; 903 P.2d 351; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 119; 69 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 56


March 9, 1994, Argued and submitted  
October 5, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] BOLI 29-90; CA A68770. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Judicial review from the Bureau of Labor and Industries. 120 Or App 273, 852 P2d 859 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The revised final order of the Bureau of Labor and Industries is reversed, and the case is remanded to that agency with instructions to dismiss the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An employee filed a complaint with petitioner, Bureau of Labor and Industries (BOLI), after he was terminated by respondent employer. BOLI found that the employer had violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.030(1)(b) by violating BOLI's implementing rule. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed BOLI's finding. BOLI petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The employer contended that BOLI's order, which stated that the employer had violated BOLI's implementing rule, violated his rights under Or. Const. art. I, §§ 2,3,5 and the First Amendment to the United States Constitution. The court concluded that the employer had established an affirmative defense under Or. Const. art. §§ 2, 3 and affirmed the appellate court's order. The court held that on its face, the implementing rule did not offend Or. Const. art. I, §§ 2, 3. However, the implementing rule offended Or. Const. art. I, §§ 2, 3 by not requiring that BOLI find that the employer know in fact that his actions in exercise of his religious practice had an effect forbidden by the rule. The court held that the employer did not know that his activities of constantly harassing the employee about his religious beliefs made the workplace intimidating, hostile, or offensive as to the employee. Therefore, the employee established an affirmative defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

Mabon v. Kulongoski


SC S42051, S42055 (Consolidated for argument and opinion)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 65; 902 P.2d 1171; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 110

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petitions to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified as modified. Decision effective pursuant to ORAP 11.30(9).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged the ballot measure certified by respondent attorney general concerning aspects of the caption, question, and summary for the ballot title for Elections Division #17, the Minority Status and Child Protection Act of 1996, under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 2-50.067(1), 250.085(2).


OVERVIEW:  Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(1)(c) required a concise and impartial statement of not more than 85 words summarizing the measure. The court found that that the ballot caption carried an unnecessary amount of rhetorical and emotion content in describing the subject of the measure. The challenge was valid as to the phrasing of the caption based on its rhetorical content and the court revised the caption. The court also found two other challenges equally valid-the inclusion in the summary of a reference to the express statutory and constitutional limitations on the scope of the measure and the revision of the treatment of the public library provision of the measure. The court rejected the contention that the summary should include a statement of the effect of the measure on local ordinances. The court certified the ballot title for the proposed initiative measure Elections Division #17 as modified.


OUTCOME: The court certified the ballot title as modified to Amends Constitution: Restricts Local, State Government Powers Concerning Homosexuality in the ballot measure challenge.

Tilbury v. Multnomah County


SC S41859


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 112; 902 P.2d 577; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 111


September 7, 1995, Argued and submitted  
September 28, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 20, 1996, Reported at: U.S. LEXIS 1043.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 3368. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  13 OTR 157 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a decision from the Oregon Tax Court, which granted summary judgment to defendant local governments, on their complaint to obtain refunds of urban renewal taxes collected by the cities and counties because those taxes exceeded the property tax limitation in Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b.


OVERVIEW: Summary judgment was granted on the basis that the taxpayers did not file their petition timely. On appeal, the taxpayers admitted that the 60-day filing period had expired prior to the time of filing, but they argued that the 60-day filing requirement of Or. Rev. Stat. § 305.583(3) was either inapplicable or unconstitutional because it could not be severed from the 10-taxpayer requirement. The court ruled that even if the 10-taxpayer rule deprived individual taxpayers their right to challenge the tax, that provision was severable from the 60-day filing requirement, and under Or. Rev. Stat. § 174.040, the requirement remained in force. Nor could the taxpayers rely on the "Taxpayer Bill Of Rights," Or. Rev. Stat. § 305.860 et seq., in attacking the adequacy of the tax notices that they received from the local governments because those statutes pertained to obligations of the Oregon Department of Revenue, not local government. Because the taxpayers' petition for refunds of urban renewal property taxes was filed too late, the lower court did not err in granting the motions for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the tax court in favor of the local governments was affirmed.

Rooney v. Kulongoski


SC S42101, S42108 (Consolidated for argument and opinion)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 77; 902 P.2d 1177; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 113


May 11, 1995, Argued and submitted

PRIOR HISTORY: On petitions to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified as modified. This decision shall become effective pursuant to ORAP 11.30(9).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters filed a ballot title proceeding challenging respondent attorney general's certified ballot title for a proposed initiative measure, arguing that the caption, question, and summary of the certified title were not in substantial compliance with the requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 250.067(1) and 250.085(2).


OVERVIEW: The voters challenged the certified ballot title, contending that it did not substantially comply with the statutes. The court found that the certified caption and question were not in compliance and revised those statements. The court ruled that the certified caption, that the measure barred public spending in ways approving homosexuality, did not reasonably identify the subject of the measure under Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(1). The court found that the caption should have characterized the common thread, rather than focusing on one aspect, and revised it to provide that the measure amended the constitution to restrict local and state government powers concerning homosexuality. The court ruled that the certified question failed to plainly phrase the chief purposes of the measure under Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(1)(b) by focusing on public funds. The court revised the question to ask whether the constitution should forbid basing civil rights on homosexuality or other sexual behaviors and bar spending public funds in ways approving homosexuality. The court concluded that the summary was correct as certified and that the phrase "right of conscience" was not inaccurate or misleading.


OUTCOME: The court found that the certified caption and question of the certified ballot title of the proposed initiative measure were not in substantial compliance with the statutory requirements for those provisions and revised the statements.

Ester v. City of Monmouth


SC S41529


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


322 Ore. 1; 903 P.2d 344; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 115


May 2, 1995, Argued and submitted  
September 28, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 3528. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  13 OTR 104 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is vacated, and the case is remanded to the Tax Court with instructions to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a decision of the Oregon Tax Court, which entered summary judgment in favor of appellee city in the taxpayer's challenge to a property assessment. The city filed a cross-appeal.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer owned two lots in the city. The property assessment was for the widening of the streets in front of the taxpayer's property. On appeal, the taxpayer argued that the special assessment against the property owners in the local improvement district was not an assessment for a "local improvement" within the meaning of the exception to the property tax limitations contained in Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b. On cross appeal, the city argued that the tax court erred in refusing to dismiss the action for lack of jurisdiction. The court agreed with the city, vacated the summary judgment order, and directed the tax court to enter an order of dismissal. In doing so, the court held that because the taxpayer did not bring her claim with at least nine other taxpayers, the tax court did not have jurisdiction to consider the claim under Or. Rev. Stat. § 305.583(1). The court further held that the 10-taxpayer requirement of § 305.583(1) did not violate due process principles, at least where the taxpayer had another avenue for review under Or. Rev. Stat. § 223.401 and where the taxpayer's challenge boiled down to nothing more than an ordinary challenge to a local improvement.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment in favor of the city was vacated in the taxpayer's challenge to the city's property assessment. The tax court was directed to enter an order of dismissal.

Boytano v. Fritz


SC S41923


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


321 Ore. 498; 901 P.2d 835; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 88


May 4, 1995, Argued and submitted  
August 24, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 93-500CV; CA A80515. On review from the Court of Appeals. * 


*  Appeal from Klamath County Circuit Court, Gayle A. Nachtigal, Judge. 128 Ore. App. 109, 875 P.2d 476, withdrawn on recons, 131 Ore. App. 466, 480, 886 P.2d 31 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the amended final order of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen challenged a decision from the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed the trial court's decision that entered judgment in favor of defendant elections officer. The citizen brought the action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief and requesting that the elections officer be prevented from placing a proposed, but not yet qualified, initiative measure on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: The citizen brought a declaratory judgment action against the elections officer, seeking to enjoin the elections officer from placing an initiative measure on the ballot. The citizen argued (1) that the proposed initiative measure should not be placed on the ballot, because it was not the kind of measure as to which initiatives were authorized by Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(5) and (2) that the proposed measure was legally insufficient on constitutional and statutory grounds. Judgment was entered in favor of the elections officer and the appellate court affirmed that decision. The citizen appealed. The court held that the case presented a justiciable controversy and found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 659.165(1) was not intended to prevent local elections on initiative petitions such as the one at issue in this case. When the legislature enacted § 659.165, it did not intend to preclude local elections on initiative measures such as the one at issue. Therefore, a local government election on an initiative measure such as the one at issue was not "contrary" to § 659.165, the general law adopted by the state legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's and trial court's decisions that rejected the citizen's argument that the proposed measure was not a proper subject of a local initiative election under the state constitution. The court held that the local government election on the initiative measure was not contrary to controlling statutory authority.

Brentmar v. Jackson County


SC S41765


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


321 Ore. 481; 900 P.2d 1030; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 93


May 3, 1995, Argued and submitted  
August 24, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] LUBA 93-208; CA A84956. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Judicial review from the Land Use Board of Appeals. 130 Ore. App. 438, 882 P.2d 1117 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The order of the Land Use Board of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded to the Land Use Board of Appeals for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant filed an application for a conditional use permit to operate an agricultural and horticultural school in an exclusive farm use (EFU) zone. The county denied his application. Respondent Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) (Oregon) affirmed the denial of the application. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed the LUBA's order. The applicant sought review.


OVERVIEW: In affirming the denial of the application, the LUBA held that the application failed to satisfy the requirements of the Jackson County, Or., Land Development Ordinance (LDO); therefore, a conditional use permit was not warranted. First, the applicant argued that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 215.213(1) and 215.283(1) required counties to treat the uses listed therein as outright permitted uses and not as conditional uses that can be subject to more stringent local criteria. Second, the applicant argued that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 215.213(2) and 215.283(2) prohibited the county from applying the more stringent LDO to the uses listed in §§ 215.213(2) and 215.283(2). In reversing the judgment, the court held that, after a review of the text, context, and legislative history of the statutes, the legislature intended that the uses delineated in §§ 215.213(1) and 215.283(1) were to be uses "as of right," which could not be subjected to additional local criteria. However, the court held that the legislature intended the uses delineated in §§ 215.213(2) and 215.283(2), were to be conditional uses; as such the county was allowed to supplement the criteria for obtaining such a permit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, thereby reversing the LUBA's affirmance of the denial of the applicant's request for a conditional use permit. The case was remanded to the LUBA for further proceedings.

Mabon v. Kulongoski


SC S42044, S. Ct. S42070 (Cases consolidated for argument and opinion)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


321 Ore. 247; 896 P.2d 574; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 47


May 11, 1995, Argued and submitted  
June 15, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petitions to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified as modified. This decision shall become effective in accordance with ORAP 11.30(9).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner electors brought an original proceeding challenging the ballot title, drafted by respondent Attorney General of Oregon, for a proposed initiative measure related to late trimester abortions.


OVERVIEW: Two sets of electors challenged the ballot title prepared by the Attorney General. The court noted that, under Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035, a ballot title was to have a caption, reasonably identifying the subject of the measure, a question, plainly phrasing the chief purpose of the measure, and a summary, concisely identifying the major effect of the measure. One set of electors challenged the question and summary portions of the ballot title, asserting that the title did not use the same words as appeared in the measure. The court found that, in this regard, the ballot title adequately conveyed the chief purpose and major effect of the measure. The other set of electors challenged all three portions of the ballot title, asserting that they failed to mention the measure's provisions regarding standing. The court agreed that the measure's grant of standing was a chief purpose and major effect of the measure and revised the question and summary portions of the ballot title to adequately refer to this portion of the measure. The court found that the caption was, however, acceptable as drafted in that it reasonably identified the measure's subject matter.


OUTCOME: The ballot title was modified in part and, as modified, certified to the Secretary of State of Oregon.

Ragsdale v. Department of Revenue


SC S41581


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


321 Ore. 216; 895 P.2d 1348; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 44; 63 U.S.L.W. 2785


March 9, 1995, Argued and submitted  
June 2, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 3535. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  13 OTR 143 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the judgment from the Oregon Tax Court, which denied her claim against respondent Department of Revenue for a refund of state income taxes paid on her federal retirement benefits for the tax year.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer sought a refund of state income taxes paid on her federal retirement income for the 1991 tax year. The taxpayer claimed that the state's system of taxation discriminated against federal retirees in violation of the intergovernmental tax immunity. Specifically, the taxpayer argued that when the state legislature voted in 1991 to increase the compensation of its retirees in the same year that it voted to remove the tax exemption on state retirement benefits, the increase in compensation amounted to a tax rebate to state retirees that federal retirees did not receive. The tax court denied her claim to a refund and the court, after de novo review, affirmed the judgment. The court held: (1) that the increase in compensation was not a tax rebate because it was paid out of the state retirement trust fund and not out of state revenue and that the increase in compensation was based on length of service and not on state income tax liability; (2) that no discrimination was shown because both federal and state retirees were taxed in like manner; and (3) that a state was not barred from increasing the compensation paid to its retirees, which increase was also subject to taxation.


OUTCOME: The court, after de novo review, affirmed the tax court's judgment that denied the taxpayer's claim for a refund.

*  Appeal from Washington County Circuit Court, Donald C. Ashmanskas, Judge. 127 Ore. App. 273, 873 P.2d 456 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration on the merits of petitioner Barcik's state law claims for retrospective relief and of the federal claims for retrospective relief of all petitioners except Kostur.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, guardians, seniors, individual senior, and ninth grader, filed an action against defendants, school district and administrators, alleging violations of the seniors', the individual senior's, and the ninth grader's constitutional rights. The guardians, the seniors, the individual senior, and the ninth grader sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a judgment in their favor.


OVERVIEW: The school district adopted regulations that permitted the administrators to censor student publications and to discipline students for violating the regulations. The seniors and the individual senior, who were involved in the publication of student newspapers, and a ninth grader alleged that the regulations violated their free speech rights. The court held (1) the appellate court erred in applying state justiciability standards to the seniors', the individual senior's, and the ninth grader's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims, (2) no justiciable controversy existed with regard to the seniors' state law claims for declaratory and injunctive relief because they alleged no facts entitling them to relief from future practices of the school district and the administrators, (3) the individual senior presented a justiciable controversy with regard to his academic record because disciplinary action had been taken against him and he was entitled to have any references to the discipline removed from his record if the administrators' actions violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8, and (4) the seniors and the individual senior presented justiciable claims for retrospective relief under § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision of the court of appeals. The court remanded the matter to the court of appeals for consideration on the merits of the individual senior's state law claims for retrospective relief and the seniors' and individual senior's federal claims for retrospective relief.

State v. Fish


SC S40015


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


321 Ore. 48; 893 P.2d 1023; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 30


August 31, 1993, Argued and submitted; November 2, 1994, reargued and resubmitted; November 3, 1994, Reassigned  
April 27, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] DC 90-11996; CA A67743. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


* Appeal from order of Clackamas County District Court, John K. Lowe, Circuit Court Judge, District Court Judge, pro tempore. 115 Ore. App. 609, 839 P.2d 278 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The order of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought to admit defendant driver's refusal to take a sobriety test into evidence at his trial for driving under the influence. The trial court granted his motion to suppress the evidence, and the Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed the order suppressing the evidence, holding that his refusal was not a violation of the right against self-incrimination.


OVERVIEW: The defendant was driving erratically and was stopped by a deputy sheriff, who asked him to perform a sobriety test and told him that his refusal could be used against him in court. The defendant refused to take the test, and was arrested for driving under the influence of intoxicants, Or. Rev. Stat. § 813.010. The trial court granted his motion to suppress evidence of his refusal on statutory and constitutional grounds. The appeals court found that the wording used by the deputy in his warning was substantially the same as that used in the administrative rule, and reversed. On further appeal, the court found that while the deputy's words conveyed the message accurately, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 813.135 and 813.136 impermissibly compelled a person to make self-incriminatory testimonial statements by either taking or refusing to take the test, the only options available under the statutes. The court held that this was a violation of the right against self-incrimination found in Or. Const. Art. I, § 12.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and affirmed the order of the district court, which had granted the defendant's motion to suppress the evidence.

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: In computing defendants' sentences, the sentencing courts used their juvenile adjudications to calculate their criminal history scores, pursuant to Or. Admin. R. 253-04-006(2). Defendants argued that because they had not been afforded jury trials in the juvenile proceedings, the use of those adjudications violated Or. Const. art. I, § 11. The court noted that defendants were being punished for their present crimes only, as to which they each received a jury trial in accordance with all constitutional requirements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions of the court of appeals and the judgments of the sentencing courts.

Atkins v. Department of Revenue


SC S41438


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 713; 894 P.2d 449; 1995 Ore. LEXIS 25


December 8, 1994, Argued and submitted; December 19, 1994, Resubmitted  
April 6, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] OTC 3469. On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge.


*  12 OTR 65 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers challenged the judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which denied their claim for tax refunds of certain state income taxes. The taxes, paid on their federal retirement income in 1986 and 1987, were assessed in violation of the constitutional doctrine of intergovernmental tax immunity by respondent Department of Revenue.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought a refund, not under Ore. Rev. Stat. § 305.765, but rather under Oregon's omnibus tax refund statute, Ore. Rev. Stat. § 305.270. The tax court had held that, under § 305.765, the taxpayers were entitled to a refund of taxes assessed and paid on their federal retirement income in or after 1988, but not before that year. The court agreed, finding that the taxpayers were not entitled to a refund of taxes invalidly collected before the 1988 tax year. Thus, the taxpayers' claim for refund of taxes paid failed, and the judgment of the tax court was affirmed. The court held that the more specific provisions of § 305.765, rather than the more general provision of § 305.270, controlled eligibility for refunds of the kind sought by the taxpayers. Further, the taxpayers were seeking a refund of taxes paid two years before any attack or challenge was made; thus their claim was barred by the limitations that the state had placed on such refunds. The court concluded that it was not required to apply § 305.270 rather than § 305.765 to the taxpayers' claim in order to provide them a refund to which they would not normally have been entitled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the tax court that denied the taxpayers' claim for refunds for taxes paid on their federal retirement income.

City Univ. v. Office of Educ. Policy & Planning


SC S41193


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 422; 885 P.2d 701; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 123


September 8, 1994, Argued and submitted  
December 16, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 88C-11645; CA A70734. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Albin W. Norblad, Judge. 126 Or App 459, 870 P2d 222 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action seeking to declare that Or. Rev. Stat. § 348.835(2)(c) was unconstitutional, plaintiff, an out-of-state private university (university) appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the portion of the judgment that held that § 348.835(2)(c) violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution, but which modified the judgment by invalidating 348.835(2)(c) in its entirety.


OVERVIEW: The university filed an action seeking to declare that § 348.835(2)(c) violated the Commerce Clause because it discriminated against out-of-state schools with branch campuses in Oregon. The trial court held that it was unconstitutional but found that the proper remedy was to delete the word "Oregon" from that paragraph. The court of appeals affirmed the finding of unconstitutionality, but it reversed the trial court's choice of remedy and invalidated § 348.835(2)(c) in its entirety. The university appealed, and the court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals. The court found that there were conflicting legislative purposes but that the dominant legislative purpose in enacting § 348.835(2)(c) was to protect Oregon citizens, educational institutions, and students from schools that entered the Oregon market with no front-end review. Such purpose was not served by severing the word "Oregon" from § 348.835(2)(c). On the other hand, if § 348.835(2)(c) was severed in its entirety, defendant Oregon Office of Educational Policy and Planning would continue to have approval and oversight authority over out-of-state schools starting satellite campuses is Oregon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that invalidated the statute on constitutional grounds.

State v. Long


SC S41205


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 361; 885 P.2d 696; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 112


September 8, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
November 25, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 33681; CA A74762. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Polk County Circuit Court, Charles E. Luukinnen, Judge. 126 Or App 126, 868 P.2d 4 (1994).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Polk County Circuit Court (Oregon), which convicted defendant of sodomy in the first degree pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.405.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted by grand jury, pursuant to Or. Const. art. VII, § 5(3), of the crime of sodomy. The indictment contained the wrong date as the date of the crime. Prior to trial, the indictment was amended, pursuant to Or. Const. art. VII, § 5(6), with defendant's full knowledge of the amendment. Defendant was convicted, and the conviction was affirmed on appeal. On further appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the mistake in the indictment was a defect of form and not of substance because the time at which the crime occurred was not a material element of the crime of sodomy. The court further determined that, because the defect was one of form, the test was whether or not defendant had been prejudiced by the amendment. The court concluded that, because defendant was aware of the amendment prior to trial, there was no prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of the crime of sodomy.

Kane v. Kulongoski


SC S41549


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 273; 882 P.2d 588; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 103


September 13, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
October 27, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified. This decision shall become effective in accordance with ORAP 11.30(9).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner elector challenged the ballot title drafted by respondent, attorney general, for a proposed initiative measure regarding the legalization of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: The elector challenged the ballot title, claming that it violated the Supremacy Clause of the United States Constitution by legalizing marijuana, which was criminalized under federal law. The court refused to rule on the constitutionality of the ballot measure at the stage in the initiative process. The elector also argued that the measure was in direct conflict with federal law, yet no part of the challenged ballot title referred to the conflict between the measure and federal law. He argued that enactment of the proposed measure would "expose" those who acted in conformance with it "to prosecution under federal law." A ballot title, the elector asserted, should have disclosed an obvious conflict between an initiative measure and federal law. The court held that the argument did not differ from the unconstitutionality argument. It was an attempt to have inserted into the ballot title a warning to the effect that the proposed measure created serious legal difficulties and questions. The elector's argument violated the requirement that a ballot title not take sides for or against a proposed measure.


OUTCOME: The court certified the ballot title.

 

62 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Nagel


SC S40605


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 24; 880 P.2d 451; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 93


April 19, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
September 9, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] DC 91-3459; CA A76823. On review from the Court of Appeals. * Resubmitted May 27, 1994.


*  Appeal from Lincoln County District Court, A. R. McMullen, Senior Judge, pro tempore. 122 Or App 638, 858 P2d 181 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon) which affirmed defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants. Defendant contended that the evidence derived from field sobriety tests should have been suppressed as violative of his rights to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII). Before trial, defendant moved to suppress all evidence derived from the field sobriety tests, arguing that the tests violated his rights to be free from unreasonable searches and seizures under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and U.S. Const. amend. IV. The officer testified that he would have arrested defendant for DUII even if defendant had refused to submit to the field sobriety tests. The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress. The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, finding that the administration of field sobriety tests were not unreasonable searches and seizures. The court concluded that, while the administration of such tests constituted a search within the meaning of Or. Const. art. I, § 9, such search was reasonable as conducted with probable cause and under exigent circumstances. The court also determined that defendant had a reasonable expectation of privacy under U.S. Const. amend. IV and that field sobriety tests violated that expectation of privacy, but that the warrantless administration of the field sobriety tests were reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals affirming defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants.

State v. Cunningham


SC S39759


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


320 Ore. 47; 880 P.2d 431; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 90


May 9, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
September 9, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Cunningham v. Oregon, 514 U.S. 1005, 115 S. Ct. 1317, 131 L. Ed. 2d 198, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 1871 (1995)
Post-conviction relief denied at Cunningham v. Thompson, 186 Ore. App. 221, 62 P.3d 823, 2003 Ore. App. LEXIS 109 (2003)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Cunningham v. Premo, 278 Ore. App. 101, 2016 Ore. App. LEXIS 539 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 91CR2626FE. On automatic and direct review of the judgment of conviction and sentence of death entered by the Circuit Court for Douglas County. Thomas Kolberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and sentence of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court for Douglas County (Oregon), which entered a jury conviction of aggravated murder and a sentence of death. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to give his requested jury instruction on manslaughter in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: After a motorist found a woman's body in a remote wooded area, defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and sentenced to death. On an automatic appeal, the court affirmed the conviction and the sentence. The trial court did not err in refusing defendant's requested jury instruction on manslaughter in the second degree because the jury could not have rationally found defendant guilty of that charge but innocent of manslaughter in the first degree where the evidence in the record overwhelmingly showed that defendant killed the victim under circumstances manifesting an extreme indifference to the value of human life. There was sufficient evidence that defendant personally and intentionally committed the homicide in the course of and in furtherance of rape in the first degree. Defendant was not entitled to statewide information under Or. Const. art. I, § 20 of other cases where defendants were charged with similar crimes and received life sentences. Defendant was not entitled to proportionality review of death sentences under U.S. Const. amend. VIII and had no federal constitutional right to obtain statewide information about similarly situated defendants for use in his trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of aggravated murder and sentence of death.

Deras v. Keisling

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voter initiated an action against respondent Secretary of State (Oregon) seeking judicial review of the proposed Oregon Campaign Finance Reform Act and contending that the explanatory statement of the new statutory enactment was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The voter initiated an action against the Secretary seeking judicial review of the proposed Oregon Campaign Finance Reform Act. The voter contended that the explanatory statement was unconstitutional and that such statement was vague and deficient. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 251.215, the explanatory statement placed in the voters' pamphlet was supposed to be an impartial, simple, and understandable statement explaining the measure. The court determined that under Or. Rev. Stat. § 251.235, it was required to review challenged explanatory statements to determine whether they were insufficient or unclear. The court further found that a statement was insufficient if it was not impartial or if it was potentially misleading. The court held that the statement was deficient in that it failed to specify whether the statutes that it purported to amend were current or subsequently amended statutes. The court held that the explanatory statement was deficient and modified the statement. The court certified the modified form of the statement.


OUTCOME: The court certified a modified form of the explanatory statement of the proposed Oregon Campaign Finance Reform Act.

State v. Stevens


SC S39427


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


319 Ore. 573; 879 P.2d 162; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 84


March 10, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
August 18, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by State v. Stevens, 325 Ore. 367, 939 P.2d 43, 1997 Ore. LEXIS 1088 (1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 88-02-0368. On automatic and direct review of the sentence of death imposed by the Circuit Court for Linn County. * William O. Lewis, Judge.


*  On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court, 311 Or 119, 806 P2d 92 (1991).


State v. Stevens, 311 Ore. 119, 806 P.2d 92, 1991 Ore. LEXIS 16 (1991)



DISPOSITION: The sentence of death is vacated. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the death sentence imposed by the Circuit Court for Linn County (Oregon).


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought a direct review of a sentence of death based upon a conviction of aggravated murder and requested that the court vacate his death sentence. Defendant had been convicted of three counts of aggravated murder. Defendant argued the trial court in his second penalty phase proceeding erred in excluding evidence that was relevant to the fourth question provided in Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.150. Defendant contended the trial court erred in sustaining the state's objection to a question that defendant's lawyer asked a state's witness on cross-examination. The court ascertained that the jury must have been able to consider and give effect to any mitigating evidence relevant to a defendant's background and character or the circumstances of the crime. Testimony by the relatives of a capital defendant may have been informative about certain aspects of the defendant's character. The court held that because the witness's testimony permitted an inference that the defendant's execution would affect his daughter negatively because of some mitigating aspect of defendant's character or background, it was relevant to the fourth question. Thus, the court vacated the sentence and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the death sentence and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

66 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Conger


SC S40908 (Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


319 Ore. 484; 878 P.2d 1089; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 81


May 13, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
August 11, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion on Remand from the Oregon Supreme Court September 27, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 1576.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC 90-10-1676-C; CA A72340. CC 91-01-1702-C; CA A71205. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeals from Harney County Circuit Court, F.J. Yraguen, Judge. State v. Conger, 125 Or App 355, 866 P2d 469, modifying 120 Or App 220, 851 P2d 636 (1993). State v. Daugherty, 125 Or App 355, 866 P2d 469 (1993), modifying 116 Or App 665, 841 P2d 704 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals on reconsideration of both cases is reversed. State v. Conger is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration of defendant's other assignments of error. The judgment of the circuit court in State v. Daugherty is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants were indicted by a grand jury of substance violations. Defendants moved to quash the indictments. A Court of Appeals (Oregon) held that an indictment found by fewer than seven grand jurors violated Or. Const. art. VII (Amended), § 5(2), and reversed and remanded the cases to the trial court with instructions to quash the indictments. The State obtained permission to consolidate and then petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The issue in the consolidated cases was whether the state constitutional provision required that seven grand jurors hear evidence to find an indictment for that indictment to be constitutionally valid. The court concluded that the framers of the Oregon Constitution in 1857 and the Oregonians who later adopted that constitution did not intend the original provision to impose a quorum requirement of seven grand jurors to find an indictment. First, in at least one instance where the framers of the Oregon Constitution intended to impose a quorum requirement to do business, it was clear from the text. Second, earlier Oregon laws on grand juries had specifically provided quorum requirements. Third, the great majority of the courts that had addressed the issue as of 1857 had concluded that the number of grand jurors required to be summoned or empaneled was not a quorum requirement. Consequently, the provision of Or. Rev. Stat. § 132.110(3) allowing at least five grand jurors to proceed upon good cause shown did not violate the current provision of the Oregon Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals on reconsideration of both cases. The court remanded one defendant's action to the court of appeals for consideration of his other assignments of error. The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court in the other defendant's action.

State v. Pinnell


SC S39190


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


319 Ore. 438; 877 P.2d 635; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 70


May 11, 1994, Argued and Submitted  
August 4, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief dismissed at Pinnell v. Palmateer, 200 Ore. App. 303, 114 P.3d 515, 2005 Ore. App. LEXIS 775 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC C88-00-26CR. On automatic and direct review of the sentence of death imposed by the Circuit Court of Washington County. * Jon B. Lund, Judge. Resubmitted and reassigned June 3, 1994.


*  On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court, 311 Or 98, 806 P2d 110 (1991).


State v. Pinnell, 311 Ore. 98, 806 P.2d 110, 1991 Ore. LEXIS 14 (1991)



DISPOSITION: The sentence of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court of Washington County (Oregon) that convicted him of aggravated murder and sentenced him to death. Defendant's appeal from the sentence of death was automatic pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.150(1)(g).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that it was error to deny his motion to dismiss the penalty-phase proceeding on remand because the proceeding did not begin in a timely manner under the speedy trial statutes and in failing to grant his motion to dismiss. The court affirmed the sentence of death, opining that the text of the speedy trial statutes, namely Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 135.760(1), 135.763(1), and 135.765, demonstrated that they applied to proceedings to determine a defendant's guilt but not to proceedings in a capital case that occurred after a court had vacated a defendant's sentence and remanded the case to the trial court for a new penalty-phase proceeding. The court concluded that it was not error to overrule defendant's motion to dismiss because a demurrer to an indictment had to be based on defects appearing on "the face" of the accusatory instrument according to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 135.630 and 135.640. The court reasoned that the error claimed by defendant involved a jury instruction that included a reference to a possible sentence that was not available to defendant. Accordingly, the court found the error to be harmless and the motion to have been properly overruled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of death, noting that the judgment of conviction for aggravated murder had been affirmed in a prior appeal.

Krieger v. Just


SC S39923, S39994


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


319 Ore. 328; 876 P.2d 754; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 66


August 31, 1993, Argued and Submitted  
July 21, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CC CV90-122; CA A69602. On review from the Court of Appeals. *


*  Appeal from Yamhill County Circuit Court, John W. Hitchcock, Judge. 117 Or App 64, 843 P2d 473 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed on different grounds. The judgment of the circuit court is reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant driver sought review of a decision of an Oregon court of appeals, which reversed the decision of the trial court granting summary judgment to the driver in a personal injury action brought by plaintiff accident victim. The driver claimed that at the time of the accident she was driving for her public employer, and that the victim did not give notice of his claim to her employer, as required under Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.275(1).


OVERVIEW: An accident victim sought damages for personal injury allegedly caused by a driver operating her personal vehicle. After the victim filed his complaint, the driver asserted that at the time of the accident she was driving for a purpose related to her public employment. The driver moved for summary judgment on the ground that the victim had not given notice to her employer of his claim, as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.275 (1). The trial court granted the motion and dismissed the victim's claim. On review, the court found that amendments to the statute required a public employee to initiate a request to his or her employer for defense and indemnification, but that the amendments do not require a plaintiff or claimant to give notice to the employer of claims against the employee. The court concluded that the legislature had no intention to grant partial immunity to public employees for their individual torts or to require notice for those torts under the Oregon Tort Claims Act. The court held that granting defendant's motion for summary judgment on the premise that the legislature had that intention was error on the part of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, reversed the decision of the trial court, and remanded the case to the trial court.

* Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Richard D. Barber, Judge. 118 Or App 735, 849 P2d 532 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of the circuit court is affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ministries, defendants, state lottery commission and director, and intervenors, restaurant and lottery consultants, sought review of the judgment by the Court of Appeals (Oregon) regarding the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 461.546(1), 461.215, 461.217 in the ministries' action for declaratory and injunctive relief, alleging that §§ 461.215, 461.217, and 461.546 violated Or. Const. art. XV, § 4.


OVERVIEW: The court had to determine if the challenged statutes violated the constitution after the court of appeals held an evidentiary hearing was required in analyzing the ambiguous terms. The court held the court of appeals erred and reversed, finding an ambiguous term, did not by itself, create an issue of fact and after considering the language, and history of the constitutional provisions, secondary treatises, and historical sources. The court considered the meaning of Or. Const. art. Const. art. XV, §§ 4(3), 4(4)(e).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals after finding that the statute regarding costs of administration was unconstitutional and that the statutes regarding casinos were constitutional.

City of Eugene v. Miller


SC No. S40396


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 480; 871 P.2d 454; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 25

April 7, 1994, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC Nos. 91-50062, 91-50073; CA Nos. A75143 (Control), A75145.


*  Appeal from Lane County District Court, Winfrid K. Liepe, Judge. 119 Or App 293, 851 P2d 1142 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed on other grounds. The judgments of conviction are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed defendant's conviction for violating one of the sidewalk vending ordinances of the city code by selling joke books on a city sidewalk.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating one of the sidewalk vending ordinances of the city code by selling joke books on a city sidewalk. The trial court reversed the convictions, concluding that the city sidewalk vending ordinance violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court concluded that the city ordinance violated § 8 because, as applied to defendant, it unreasonably impinged on the dissemination of expressive material that was itself protected under § 8. The court held that the principle that expressive material was not exempt from "content-neutral" regulation did not mean, as the city contended, that expressive material could be subjected to any "content-neutral" regulation that the city wished to impose. The court held that § 8, required that defendant have the same opportunity for the public sale of his expressive material goods that was given to vendors of other products. The court held that the ordinance under which defendant was convicted unconstitutionally denied defendant that opportunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision which reversed defendant's conviction for violating one of the sidewalk vending ordinances of the city code by selling joke books on a city sidewalk.

 

72 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Emery


SC No. S40000


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 460; 869 P.2d 859; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 20


November 4, 1993, Argued and submitted; March 22, 1994, Resubmitted  
March 24, 1994, Decided  
March 24, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * DC No. L90-0163CR; CA No. A69343.


*  Appeal from Lake County District Court, Wayne H. Blair, Senior Judge pro tempore (motion to dismiss); Roxanne Osborne, Judge (amended judgment). 115 Or App 655, 840 P2d 95 (1992), modified on recon 117 Or App 565, 842 P2d 467 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The judgment of conviction is reversed, and the charge against defendant is dismissed pursuant to ORS 135.747.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that held defendant was not denied his right to a speedy trial under Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.747 or under Or. Const. art. I, § 10. Defendant had been convicted of possession of an elk, a misdemeanor under Or. Rev. Stat. § 496.992(1) and Or. Admin. Code § 635-65-765(8).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the court of appeals judgment that affirmed his conviction for possession of an elk on the grounds that defendant was required to show prejudice in order to prove he was denied his right to a speedy trial under Or. Const. art. I, § 10 and Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.747. The state conceded that the delay was attributable to the state. The court reversed the appeals court judgment. The court held that § 135.747 did not require a showing of prejudice. The court held that where the case was not brought to trial within a reasonable period of time and was not delayed by defendant or with his consent, the trial court was required to dismiss the case, regardless of whether defendant was prejudiced. The court noted that dismissal under § 135.747 was without prejudice and allowed reprosecution by the state. The court found that under the constitution, dismissal with prejudice was required when there had been intentional prosecution delay or probable prejudice to the defense. The court held that defendant was not entitled to dismissal with prejudice under the constitution, because he conceded that the delay was not intentional and failed to show sufficient prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals judgment that held defendant was not denied his right to a speedy trial. The court held that defendant was entitled to dismissal without prejudice under the statutory provisions for speedy trial, but was not entitled to dismissal with prejudice under the Constitution. The court reversed defendant's conviction for possession of an elk and dismissed the charge against defendant.

Palmer v. State


SC No. S40704


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 352; 867 P.2d 1368; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 13


January 3, 1994, Argued and submitted  
February 25, 1994, Decided  
February 25, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 16-90-06124; CA No. A71861.


*  Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court, Maurice K. Merten, Judge. 121 Or App 377, 854 P2d 955 (1993).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed, in part on different grounds. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought relief from his conviction of unlawful possession of a firearm in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.250, contending that the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.510 allowed him to raise an issue that was not raised at the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of unlawful possession of a firearm, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.250, for possession of a concealed handgun. The appellate court affirmed his conviction, and the court denied review. Subsequently, defendant instituted an action that sought relief from his conviction under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.510 to 138.680, contending that he was entitled to post conviction relief because Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.250, was unconstitutionally vague, due to its failure to define the word concealed. The court held that the issue was whether defendant might assert as a ground for post-conviction relief an issue that was not raised at trial in the underlying criminal proceeding, when defendant reasonably could have been expected to raise that issue in the trial court, and when defendant did not assert that the failure to raise that issue constituted inadequate assistance of trial counsel. The court concluded that defendant might not assert such an issue as a ground for post-conviction relief when he failed to make it an issue at the trial court level.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for unlawful possession of a firearm.

 

74 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Multnomah County Bd. of Comm'rs


SC No. S40152


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 302; 865 P.2d 356; 1994 Ore. LEXIS 4


November 1, 1993, Argued and submitted  
January 20, 1994, Decided  
January 20, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. Carl N. Byers, Judge. OTC No. 3329.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the decision of the Oregon Tax Court, which granted judgment for respondent county board of commissioners in the taxpayer's lawsuit seeking a refund, under the general refund statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 311.806, of taxes imposed and collected in excess of the limits of Or. Const. art. XI, § 11b (Measure 5).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer owned real property in the city. The taxes assessed on that property included an amount imposed for the repayment of the city's urban renewal bonds. On the taxpayer's tax statement, that figure was represented as excluded from the property tax limits imposed by Measure 5. The taxpayer paid the taxes in full. Afterwards, the court held that taxes imposed for the repayment of urban renewal bonds were not exempt from the limits of Measure 5. The taxpayer sought a refund, under § 311.806, of taxes imposed and collected in the excess of Measure 5. The court affirmed the trial court's decision holding that §§ 305.583 and 305.587 provided the exclusive means for obtaining such a refund. Under § 305.587 the Tax Court had authority to order a governmental unit to make refunds to the petitioners in a proceeding brought under § 305.583, if it found that the challenged tax was subject to the limits of Measure 5. Because §§ 305.587 and 305.583 were specific statutes, their special provisions prevailed over the general statutes for refunds. Only under §§ 305.587 and 305.583 could the appropriate governmental unit be responsible for refunding excess taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the tax court's decision.

 

75 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


deParrie v. Keisling


SC Nos. S40633, S40609 (Cases Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 62; 862 P.2d 494; 1993 Ore. LEXIS 160


October 19, 1993, Argued and submitted  
November 26, 1993, Decided  
November 26, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petitions to review a ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified as modified. This decision shall become effective pursuant to ORAP 11.30(10).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner litigants and respondent Secretary of State, State of Oregon, were before the court on the litigants' petitions seeking review of a ballot title. The litigants', who voiced objections to the Secretary and remained dissatisfied with the ballot title certified by the attorney general, sought a different ballot title from the court.


OVERVIEW: The litigants asserted that the ballot title certified by the attorney general did not comply with Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035. A main thrust of the initiative was to forbid governments from passing laws that established legally protected homosexuality classifications. The court held that the attorney general's caption did not reasonably identify the subject of the measure and, therefore, did not comply with § 250.035(1)(a). The court held the main thrust of the initiative was not to allow the passage of "anti-homosexual laws." The court modified the caption to read: Amends Constitution. Declares Homosexuality Wrongful. Forbids Homosexuality as Protected Status. The court held the question of the initiative failed in part to comply with § 250.035(1)(b). The court held the focus of the question should have included the declaration that homosexuality was wrongful sexual behavior. The court modified the question. The court held that the summary of the initiative did not substantially comply with § 250.035(1)(c). The court modified the summary so as to comply with § 250.035(1)(c). The court ordered its decision was to become effective pursuant to Or. R. App. P. 11.30(10).


OUTCOME: The court modified the caption, the question, and the summary of the ballot title. The court certified the ballot title as modified.

Alsea Veneer, Inc. v. State


SC Nos. S40047, S40048


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


318 Ore. 33; 862 P.2d 95; 1993 Ore. LEXIS 159


September 1, 1993, Argued and submitted  
November 18, 1993, Decided  
November 18, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied March 8, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 88C-11289; CA No. A68787 (Control); No. 88C-11300; CA No. A68788.


*  Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Rodney W. Miller, Judge. 117 Or App 42, 843 P2d 492 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The judgments of the circuit court are reversed, and the cases are remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, employers that were insured by the State Accident Insurance Fund Corporation (SAIF), sought review of a judgment from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which reversed the trial court's dismissal of the employers' class action, denied the employers' request for equitable relief, and entered judgment in favor of defendant, the State of Oregon (state), in an action to require the state to return funds to the Insurance Accident Fund (IAF).


OVERVIEW: The employers filed a class action against the state for mandatory equitable relief to force the state to return funds that were unlawfully transferred from the IAF to the general fund. The trial court denied the employers' motion for class certification, dismissed the employers' claim for equitable relief, and entered judgment in favor of the state. The court of appeals reversed the trial court's judgment but denied the employers' request for equitable relief. The state claimed on appeal that the court of appeals erred in certifying the class and that the employers should have been limited to their remedies at law. The court rejected the state's claims. The court found that class certification was appropriate and that an action for damages would not, as a practical matter, compensate the employers for the losses that they incurred by depletion of the IAF fund, that the employers were entitled to repayment of the funds by the state, and to an equitable distribution of the funds as determined by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeal's certification of the employers' class. The court reversed the court of appeals judgment that denied the employers equitable relief in the distribution of IAF fund and remanded the action for repayment and distribution of the funds that were unlawfully transferred by the state from the IAF into the general fund.

Mabon v. Keisling


SC Nos. S40331, S40316 (Cases Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


317 Ore. 406; 856 P.2d 1023; 1993 Ore. LEXIS 128


July 27, 1993, Argued and submitted  
August 19, 1993, Decided  
August 19, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 3, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: On petitions to review ballot title.



DISPOSITION: Ballot title certified as modified. This decision shall become effective pursuant to ORAP 11.30(10).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The proceeding consolidated two challenges to a ballot title certified by the attorney general for a proposed initiative measure that would add a new section to article I of the Oregon Constitution. Petitioners previously submitted to the secretary of state timely written comments pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.067(1), and consequently were entitled to bring the challenges to the certified ballot title, Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.085(2), (5).


OVERVIEW: Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.085(4), the court reviewed ballot tiles for substantial compliance with Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 250.035, 250.039. Under Or. Rev. Stat. 250.035(1)(a) the caption was not to have more than 10 words that reasonably identified the subject of the measure. The court concluded that the caption did not speculate in describing what the measure may have prevented. The court held that if enacted, the impact would have been broader than the effect emphasized in the caption. However, the court held that it was preferable not to include a phrase in the caption, by putting it in quotation marks. Thus, the court modified the caption. Petitioners next contended that Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035(1)(b), which required a question of not more than 20 words that plainly phrased the chief purpose, was not complied with because the question reached beyond the unambiguous language of the measure and assumed secondary effects. Although the court did not agree, the court concluded that effect the attorney general chose to emphasize was the most significant aim or end which the measure was designed to bring about. Thus, the court certified the ballot title as modified.


OUTCOME: In petitioners' actions that challenged the ballot title certified by the attorney general the court certified the ballot title as modified.

Huffman & Wright Logging Co. v. Wade


SC No. S38633


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


317 Ore. 445; 857 P.2d 101; 1993 Ore. LEXIS 132


May 4, 1992, Argued and submitted; August 10, 1993, Reassigned  
August 19, 1993, Decided  
August 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. L87-2850; CA No. A61324.


* Appeal from Douglas County Circuit Court, Hugh C. Downer, Jr., Judge. 109 Or App 37, 817 P2d 1334 (1991).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury awarded respondent logging corporation (corporation) punitive damages in its action against petitioner environmental group members (members) for trespass to chattels. The Douglas County Circuit Court (Oregon) entered judgment on the jury's verdict, and the court of appeals affirmed. The members challenged the award of punitive damages on constitutional grounds.


OVERVIEW: The action stemmed out of a demonstration, whereby the members chained themselves to the corporation's logging equipment, rendering the same inoperable. The court held that the award of punitive damages did not violate the members right to free speech under Oregon Const. art. I, § 8 and the First and Fourteenth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution, because their trespassory acts were "non-expressive." In so ruling, the court noted the jury could have found that the members' actions caused a disturbance of the corporation's possession of its personal property, wholly apart from any motivating opinion, underlying message, or accompanying speech. Further, the members could have requested a jury instruction limiting the tortious predicate for punitive damages to conduct not protected by the free speech, but failed to do so. The court held that the award of punitive damages did not interfere with the members' right to assemble in violation of Oregon Const. art. I, § 26, because such right did not include the right to disrupt another's possession of private property and the members' acts were not a petition to the government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Cope v. Cannon Beach

*  Judicial review from Land Use Board of Appeals. 115 Or App 11, 14, 836 P2d 775 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the order of the Land Use Board of Appeals are affirmed on other grounds.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff rental property owners sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon) awarding judgment to defendant city in an action challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance regulating the occupancy of rental properties.


OVERVIEW: The rental property owners filed an action against the city, challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance regulating the occupancy of rental properties. The owners contended that the ordinance constituted a taking of their property without just compensation, in violation of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. The lower court upheld the ordinance, holding that the owners could not establish a taking until they applied for and were denied the hardship relief provided in the ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the ordinance did not violate the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. The court further found that the ordinance substantially advanced legitimate governmental interests. The interests in securing affordable housing for permanent residents and in preserving the character and integrity of residential neighborhoods were legitimate governmental interests, and the challenged ordinance had a substantial nexus to the achievement of those aims. Finally, the court found that the ordinance did not deprive the owners of economically viable use of their properties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order awarding judgment to the city in the rental property owners' action challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance regulating the occupancy of rental properties.

Dodd v. Hood River County

*  Judicial review from Land Use Board of Appeals, 22 LUBA 771 (1992). 115 Or App 139, 836 P2d 1373 (1992).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowners sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the land use appeals board's (board) decision. The board had affirmed respondent county's decision to deny the landowners' application for a conditional use permit to build a single-family dwelling in a forestry zone.


OVERVIEW: The landowners purchased 40 acres of land for $ 33,000 on which they planned to build a house when they retired. At that time, the zoning of the land permitted construction of a dwelling, but the county was in the process of revising its comprehensive plan to bring it in conformity with statewide goals. The following year a county ordinance was amended to prohibit the construction of a dwelling of forest zone land 40 acres or larger. The landowners filed with the county an application for a land use permit, conditional use permit, and a zone and comprehensive plan change, but the county denied the application. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the board's decision was supported by substantial evidence, as the evidence showed that the landowners' property had a potential value of at least $ 10,000. The board did not err in concluding that the land was not "taken" by the county in violation of Ore. Const. art. I, § 18, because the county's zoning ordinances allowed the landowners some substantial beneficial use of their property. The landowners had at least constructive notice at the time they purchased the land of pending zoning limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals that found that the board's decision to deny the landowners' application for a conditional use permit was supported by substantial evidence.
