Employment Div. v. Rogue Valley Youth for Christ


SC No. S34864


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


307 Ore. 490; 770 P.2d 588; 1989 Ore. LEXIS 21


May 3, 1988, Argued and submitted  
May 7, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** ED No. 85-T-191; CA No. A41242.


**  Judicial review from an order of the Employment Division. 87 Or App 573, 743 P2d 745 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The final order of the Employment Division referee is reversed and the case is remanded to the Employment Division for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner on review Employment Division, Department of Human Resources (department) sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which entered a judgment for respondent on review church in the action by the department to assess unemployment compensation taxes on the church.


OVERVIEW: The church was notified that it would be required to pay unemployment payroll taxes. At a hearing requested by the church, the referee determined that respondent on review was a church, and was therefore exempt from unemployment compensation. The department sought judicial review of the referee's decision and argued that it could constitutionally distinguish between churches and other religious organizations but must tax them all. The appellate court entered a judgment for the church because it determined there was no actual controversy. The department appealed and the court determined that it could consider the constitutional issue because there was an actual controversy. The court reversed and determined that distinguishing between churches and other religious organization for the purposes of unemployment taxation would violate the freedom of religion clauses of Or. Const. art. 1, §§ 2 and 3. The court found that the legislature intended to comply with the requirements of the Federal Unemployment Tax Act to avoid double taxation of employers. The court concluded that the requiring all churches to pay unemployment taxes would not violate Oregon's free exercise of religion clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and the referee, and held that the department could require the church to pay unemployment compensation taxes. The court remanded the decision to the department for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

The court did not decide whether the evidence would ultimately support a claim for habeas corpus relief, but the court held that the inmate had a right to a hearing. 171 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. McKenzie


SC No. S35592


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


307 Ore. 554; 771 P.2d 264; 1989 Ore. LEXIS 112


January 31, 1989, Argued and submitted  
March 21, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. C87-02-30673; CA No. A44952.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Honorable James R. Ellis, Judge. 92 Or App 647, 759 P2d 1123 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed defendant's conviction for escape in the second degree without considering defendant's claim that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.135(4) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was released from incarceration as an inmate after taking advantage of the facilities' mistaken belief that he was the inmate that a security deposit had been received for. He was subsequently rearrested and charged with escape in the second degree. At the conclusion of the State's case, defendant's attorney moved for a judgment of acquittal alleging that the words "unlawful departure" as used in § 162.135(4) defining "escape" were unconstitutionally vague. The trial judge considered the motion on its merits and denied it, and the appellate court held that such a challenge was not proper and affirmed defendant's conviction without reaching the merits. The court concluded that although a motion for a judgment of acquittal was not the proper method to challenge the constitutionality of the statute, a remedy was available in the form of a motion in arrest of judgment. The court held that it was inappropriate to construe any motion for a judgment of acquittal as a demurrer if the motion was made after jeopardy had attached. Also, the court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.640 did not require that a vagueness challenge be made only by demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment affirming defendant's conviction and remanded to the appellate court for it to decide the vagueness claim, and for the appellate court to treat the appeal as one from the denial of a motion in arrest of judgment.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a condition of probation imposed after his conviction for custodial interference.

State v. Hitz

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


307 Ore. 183; 766 P.2d 373; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 769


October 4, 1988, Argued and submitted  
December 20, 1988

*  Appeal from judgment of the Marion County District Court, H. William Barlow, Judge. 92 Or App 136, 757 P2d 448 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the district court are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting her of knowing unlawful misapplication of entrusted property under Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.095(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an insurance agent, was charged with intentionally misapplying and disposing of monies received as premiums toward an insurance policy that she did not forward in full to the insurance company. The mens rea requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166 included: (1) knowledge that the conduct or action was contrary to the legally established rules governing care of entrusted property; and (2) knowledge that such conduct or action involved a substantial risk of loss or detriment to the actual owner or beneficiary of the property; and (3) an intentional misapplication or disposition of that property. Defendant's testimony was that she was ill and her office disorganized and that she mislaid the application and could not reconstruct how much needed to be forwarded. Defendant argued that the State had not proved her knowledge that any misapplication of funds was done "unlawfully;" and had not proved an element of misapplication required by the statute, that is, violation of a law or regulation outside Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.095. The court held that the State made no effort to show such a violation and that defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the appellate court and the trial court convicting defendant of knowing unlawful misapplication of entrusted property.

State v. Gallant


SC No. S34952


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


307 Ore. 152; 764 P.2d 920; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 757


June 7, 1988, Argued and submitted  
November 30, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. B69-226; CA No. A45156.


**  Appeal from the District Court of Lane County, Frank R. Alderson, Judge. 89 Or App 264, 749 P2d 141 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction in the trial court for second degree theft for shoplifting under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 164.015(1), 164.045.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that his prior conviction for second degree theft for shoplifting was not admissible as a crime involving dishonesty, the amendments to Or. Evid. Code § 609 were ex post facto, and that the trial court should have granted defendant a mistrial when the prosecution asked questions about his prior conviction for shoplifting. The court affirmed the appellate court judgment, which had affirmed the trial court conviction of defendant, holding that the shoplifting conviction involved dishonesty, and under Or. Evid. Code § 609(1)(b), defendant's prior conviction was admissible to impeach defendant when he testified. Further, the modification of § 609(1)(b) was not ex post facto, because the changes did not create a new crime or require any additional proof by defendant. The court held that although it was error for the prosecution to inquire into the prior conviction, which the prosecution admitted, the error was not prejudicial to defendant, since the jury already knew about the shoplifting conviction, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion by denying the mistrial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision and the trial court's conviction of defendant for second degree theft for shoplifting.

307 Ore. 1; 762 P.2d 290; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 549


January 19, 1988, Argued and submitted  
September 30, 1988

*  Appeal from Lane County District Court, Laurie K. Smith, Judge. 87 Or App 552, 743 P2d 185 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the trial court and the Court of Appeals are reversed. This case is remanded to the trial court to vacate the Order of Probation and impose sentence upon the defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which sentenced defendant to probation for his conviction on the charge of initiating a false report to the police.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of initiating a false report to the police by falsely reporting that his car was stolen. Defendant was sentenced to probation, but he wished to be sentenced to jail time as he did not believe he would be successful in the supervised situation of probation. The lower court affirmed the probation. Upon appeal, the court reversed and remanded to vacate the probation order and to impose a sentence on defendant. The court found that probation was not a sentence and defendant had the right to appeal pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. 138.040. Therefore, the order of probation was appealable as a judgment on conviction and the appeal or review was not limited to whether it exceeded the maximum allowable by law or was unconstitutionally cruel and unusual as in the case of a sentence. By giving the trial courts authority to impose special conditions of probation, which could have invaded defendant's constitutional rights, the legislature gave defendant the right to an election to accept or reject probation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the imposition of probation on defendant. The court remanded for the imposition of a sentence on defendant.

State v. Edgmand


SC No. S35081


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 535; 761 P.2d 505; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 538


July 14, 1988, Argued and submitted  
September 20, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. CF 87-102; CA No. A44344.


*  Appeal from judgment of Umatilla County Circuit Court, Robert B. Abrams, Judge. 90 Or App 193, 751 P2d 1129 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are reversed, and the case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed her conviction for unlawful delivery and unlawful possession of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant had sought to testify in her own defense that she was at another location at the time when the State's witness bought methamphetamine at the house where defendant lived. The State had objected on the grounds that defendant's testimony would constitute "alibi evidence" requiring prior notice to the district attorney pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.455, which defendant had not given. The trial court sustained the objected, defendant was subsequently convicted, and her conviction was upheld on appeal to the intermediate appellate court. On review, defendant contended that the statute was unconstitutional if it was applied to restrict what a defendant, who had not given prior notice, might say in her own defense. The court noted that § 135.455 was designed to prevent the unanticipated appearance of surprise alibi witnesses for a defendant and to give the State the opportunity to meet their testimony, and not to deny a defendant's right to describe her own movements at the time in question. Accordingly, the circuit court should not have excluded defendant's statements. The court therefore reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' decision to uphold defendant's controlled substance convictions as well as the judgment of conviction from the circuit court, and remanded the matter to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

180 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Hillsboro v. Purcell


SC No. S34643 (Cases Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 547; 761 P.2d 510; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 540


March 2, 1988, Argued and submitted  
September 20, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 85-0424, 85-0425; CA Nos. A41670 (Control), A41671.


*  Appeal from judgments of Washington County Circuit Court, Jon B. Lund, Judge. 87 Or App 649, 743 P2d 1119 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The decision of the circuit court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the convictions of defendant salesmen in circuit court for selling household products door-to-door in violation of Hillsboro, Or., Ordinance No. 2488-2-72, § 1 (1972). The appellate court ruled that the statute under which the salesmen were convicted violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8.


OVERVIEW: The salesmen were initially convicted in municipal court of selling household goods door-to-door and fined. The circuit court consolidated the cases and in a trial de novo affirmed the convictions. On further appeal, the appellate court reversed the convictions, holding that Hillsboro, Or. Ordinance No. 24-2-72, §1 was overbroad and violated the state constitution. The city challenged the appellate court's reversal on appeal, and the court affirmed, holding that: 1) although the ordinance was not an outright prohibition on speech, it was overbroad because it prohibited all solicitation for any purpose at any time; 2) the broad language of the ordinance failed to narrow its reach to regulate specific conduct, and instead proscribed all conduct related to a particular activity; 3) speech was not properly prohibited by criminal or civil regulation; 4) the court was unable to narrow the language of the ordinance within constitutional limits because it was unable to determine the intended limits of the ordinance; and 5) while the city was permitted to place reasonable limits on home solicitations, a total prohibition on such activity was impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court reversing the convictions of defendants for selling household goods door-to-door.

Hunter v. State


SC No. S34130 Cases Consolidated


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 529; 761 P.2d 502; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 524


November 4, 1987, Argued and submitted  
September 7, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 38367, 38667; CA Nos. A37983 (Control), A37984.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Deschutes County, Walter I. Edmonds, Jr., Judge. 84 Or App 698, 735 P2d 1225 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the circuit court dismissals are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the judgment of the circuit court and found that petitioners were given the right to seek post-conviction relief pursuant to the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 138.510-138.680 following convictions for violating municipal ordinances.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners pled guilty to and were convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII) in violation of the Bend Uniform Traffic Code. Following the convictions, petitioners sought post-conviction relief under the Act, but their cases were dismissed by the circuit court. The appellate court reversed on the ground that the phrase "under the laws of this State," which appeared in Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.510(1), included municipal ordinances. The court reversed and held that those convicted of violating municipal ordinances had not been "convicted of a crime under the laws of the State," and were not entitled to use the procedures available under the Act. The court interpreted that § 138.510(1) was meant to apply only to laws enacted by the State government, not by a municipality. The court further ruled that denying petitioners the opportunity to seek relief pursuant to the Act was not in violation of the equal protection clause of Or. Const. art. I, § 20, because the distinction of those convicted under municipal ordinances and those convicted of a State crime was created by statute rather than by personal or immutable characteristics.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court and affirmed the dismissals of petitioners' actions for post-conviction relief under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act.

Mt. Sexton Properties, Inc. v. Department of Revenue


SC No. S34654


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 465; 760 P.2d 1320; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 528


June 7, 1988, Argued and submitted  
August 30, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 29, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge. OTC No. 2510.


*  10 OTR 467 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed from a decision of the state Tax Court which denied their request for declaratory relief in an action against respondent state challenging the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 321.352(5).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers owned several parcels of forest land. The taxpayers received notices of land valuation increases leading to significant tax assessment increases. The taxpayers did not appeal the increases, and they paid the taxes assessed against their property. The taxpayers brought an action against the state seeking declaratory judgment challenging the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 321.352(5), which provided for administration determination of the value of forest land with an opportunity of appeal when the value was promulgated, claiming that they were not given adequate notice of an opportunity to join litigation concerning the valuation increases. The tax court held in favor of the state, and the taxpayers appealed. The court ruled that due process did not require that every potentially affected party be notified prior to the initiation of procedures established by a legislative scheme even if the eventual outcome was a property tax assessment. The court affirmed the judgment, concluding that due process was satisfied if the property owner was given an opportunity to be heard prior to the imposition of the assessment as a charge on the land.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying the declaratory relief to the taxpayers was affirmed.

 

183 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


First Interstate Bank, N.A. v. Department of Revenue


SC No. S34361


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 450; 760 P.2d 880; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 533


April 6, 1988, Argued and submitted  
August 30, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of the Oregon Tax Court. * Carl N. Byers, Judge. OTC No. 2515.


*  10 OTR 452 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer, a bank, challenged the decision of the Oregon Tax Court, which ruled in favor of respondent, the Oregon Department of Revenue, and determined that it was necessary to assess each of the taxpayer's lots individually to ascertain the true cash value of the properties as required by Oregon's ad valorem taxation laws.


OVERVIEW: The county assessor appraised the value of the taxpayer's 32 lots and a separate tract, and the tax court upheld the valuation. The main issue on review before the court was whether each property was required to be assessed as a separate unit or whether the properties could be assessed as a single property, which would result in a lower assessed value. The court affirmed the assessment. The court held that each of the 32 lots and the tract was a separate tax lot. Each lot was required to be individually assessed to ascertain its true cash value under the state's ad valorem taxation laws. The court rejected the taxpayer's argument that the developer's discount was the proper method of valuation because it did not achieve the fair market value. The taxpayer, as the challenging party, had the burden to show that a correct appraisal depended on the adoption of its preferred method of valuation. By relying on an improper assessment method, the taxpayer failed to show that its assessment was correct. The taxpayer failed to meet its burden of showing that the assessed value as determined by the tax court was incorrect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the tax court and held that each property was required to be individually assessed.

Koch v. Portland

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 444; 760 P.2d 252; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 534


May 4, 1988, Argued and submitted  
August 30, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. A8509-05763; CA No. A39374.


*  Appeal from judgment of Multnomah County Circuit Court, Stephen L. Gallagher, Jr., Judge. 88 Or App 335, 745 P2d 435 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration of the merits of the issues argued by the parties.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee, a police officer, sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a judgement in favor of defendant city in an action by the employee for a writ of review under Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.100, which challenged the employee's suspension by the mayor of the city but ruled that it lacked writ of review jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a police officer for the city. The employee had a conversation with a public figure regarding the conduct of two other officers in a well-publicized controversy. The investigative division of the police department investigated the matter and determined that the employee's conduct violated the department's rule against publicizing the department's policies, programs, actions, or officers. The investigative division recommended that the employee be suspended for 10 days. The mayor of the city ordered the suspension in his capacity as the commissioner of public safety. The employee filed an action for a writ of review from the suspension. The trial court denied the writ on its merits. The appeals court determined that the mayor's decision was administrative and that it lacked jurisdiction over the writ because the writ was available only for review of judicial or quasi-judicial acts. The employee claimed on appeal that the mayor's decision was quasi-judicial. The city agreed with the employee's claim. The court ruled that the decision was quasi-judicial because the process concerned application of a pre-existing criterion to the facts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court's ruling that it lacked jurisdiction over the employee's action for a writ of review from the employee's suspension by the city and remanded the action for consideration of the merits of the issues.

Eckles v. State


SC No. S32710


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 380; 760 P.2d 846; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 447


September 2, 1986, Argued and submitted; March 16, 1988, Reassigned  
August 4, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 29, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 143089; CA No. A35776.


* Appeal from judgment of Marion County Circuit Court, Duane R. Ertsgaard, Judge. 77 Or App 726, 714 P2d 1119 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed in part and reversed in part. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employer filed a motion for declaratory and injunctive relief on the grounds that the Transfer Act (Act), 1982 Or. Laws Spec. Sess. 3, which directed defendant state to transfer money from the Industrial Accident Fund (IAF) to the General Fund, violated Or. Const. art. I, § 21. The complaint was dismissed and judgment entered for defendant. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employer contended that the Act was illegal; that the transfer of funds deprived him of property rights in the IAF; that the Act impaired his insurance contract with the State Accident Insurance Fund Corporation (SAIF); that the transfer violated the takings clause of the Or. Const. art. I, § 18; and that the Act deprived him of due process under Or. Const. art. I, § 10. The state argued that the employer had no vested rights in the IAF and that the transfer did not impair his contract with SAIF. The supreme court reversed in part, concluding that (1) the transfer breached a contract of the state, but that it did not violate the state or federal constitutions. (2) The Act, 1982 Or. Laws Spec. Sess. 4, which retroactively amended Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.634, violated Or. Const. art. I, § 21. (3) The employer's allegation that the transfer of funds from the IAF affected his rights was sufficient to establish standing under Or. Rev. Stat. § 28.020. (4) The employer's takings and due process arguments failed because he failed to show a taking or deprivation, and failed to show that the transfer of funds harmed his contractual interests.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment as to the state's claim that a transfer of funds into the general fund did not violate the constitution. The court reversed the judgment as to the state's claim that the transfer was not a breach of contract. The court remanded the case to the lower court for further proceedings.

State v. Campbell


SC No. S34651


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 157; 759 P.2d 1040; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 400


May 5, 1988, Argued and submitted  
July 12, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 85-164, 85-165; CA No. A37511.


*  Appeal from judgment of Clackamas County Circuit Court, Patrick D. Gilroy, Judge. 87 Or App 415, 742 P2d 683 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the circuit court and the decision of the Court of Appeals are affirmed. The case is remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a trial court's judgment that granted defendant's motion to suppress in defendant's trial for burglary. The lower court held that the police monitoring of a radio transmitter that was attached to defendant's car by police without a warrant was a seizure under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and that all evidence derived from the use of the transmitter was properly suppressed.


OVERVIEW: After suspecting that defendant was committing burglaries, police officers attached a radio transmitter to defendant's car without obtaining a warrant. After defendant was indicted for burglary, the trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress all evidence derived from the use of the transmitter. The lower court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that a search or seizure to obtain evidence of a crime was unconstitutional if no warrant authorized the search or seizure and there was no exigency that obviated the need for a warrant. In defining searches under Or. Const. art. I, § 9, the court rejected the terms "reasonable expectations of privacy" as used by the United States Supreme Court in defining searches under the Fourth Amendment. The court held that the use of the transmitter to locate defendant's car was a search under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 and that the trial court properly suppressed the evidence because the police did not have a warrant to use the transmitter and no exigency obviated the need to obtain a warrant. The court did not decide whether the attachment and monitoring of the transmitter was a "seizure" under Or. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that affirmed the trial court's judgment, which granted defendant's motion to suppress in defendant's trial for burglary with respect to all evidence derived from the use of a radio transmitter that was attached to defendant's car by police without a warrant.

Portland v. Tidyman


SC No. S34556


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 174; 759 P.2d 242; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 401; 57 U.S.L.W. 2060


January 20, 1988, Argued and submitted  
July 12, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. A8305-03194, A8305-03190, A8305-03192; CA No. A34453.


*  Appeal from judgment of the Circuit Court for Multnomah County, William Riggs, Judge. 87 Or App 488, 745 P2d 1225 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, City of Portland, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the circuit court's determination that denied the city's case to enjoin, as a public nuisance, defendant adult bookstores from operating in certain locations where Portland, Or., Mun. Ordinance No. 155387 banned adult bookstores.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted Portland, Or., Mun. Ordinance No. 155387, which required "adult businesses" to locate at least 500 feet distant from any residential zone or any public or private school and, in some zones, at least 1000 feet from any other adult business. The city sought to enjoin the bookstores from operating in violation of the ordinance. At trial, the circuit court found that the ordinance was unconstitutional under Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and the appellate court affirmed. The court affirmed the decision and found that the ordinance, although modeled after one that had survived a First Amendment challenge, prohibited the bookstores' material based upon its content and therefore was subject to strict scrutiny which it did not meet. The court found that the ordinance did not prohibit the explicit material based upon effects of the speech, which might have been constitutional, but rather prohibited the content without having had any reference to any negative or nuisance effect demonstrated. The city's legislative findings did not overcome the city's burden to establish the negative effects of the bookstores.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that the city's ordinance was unconstitutional.

Pooler v. Motor Vehicles Div.


SC No. S34832


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


306 Ore. 47; 755 P.2d 701; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 256


April 6, 1988, Argued and submitted  
June 7, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 85-547-CV; CA No. A41560.


*  On appeal from Circuit Court, Klamath County, Theodore D. Abram, Judge. 88 Or App 475, 746 P2d 716 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the trial court and the Court of Appeals are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Motor Vehicles Division (Oregon) appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed an order vacating the suspension of respondent driver's operator's license for driving while under the influence of intoxicants (DUII), in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540.


OVERVIEW: The driver made a U-turn to evade a state sobriety roadblock. In response to this avoidance maneuver, an officer stopped the driver's car and noticed a smell of alcohol. The driver was arrested after completing field sobriety tests. He took a chemical breath test, which measured his blood-alcohol level to be above legal limits. The Division suspended his driver's license. The driver sought an administrative appeal hearing, at which the suspension was affirmed. The appellate court affirmed the circuit court's decision to vacate the administrative suspension. The court affirmed that judgment, holding that the administrative hearing officers had a duty under Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.541 to determine if the arrest was valid. The legislative intent behind § 482.541 was that an "arrest" was to mean a valid arrest. The arrest was clearly invalid given the Division's concession that the investigatory stop was not lawful. Therefore, any evidence that was obtained as a result of the stop was inadmissible and the officers did not have probable cause to arrest the driver.


OUTCOME: In the Division's appeal, the court affirmed the judgment that affirmed the order that vacated the suspension of the driver's license.

 

189 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Eugene v. Roberts


SC No. S35155


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


305 Ore. 641; 756 P.2d 630; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 240


May 13, 1988, Argued and submitted  
May 17, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 16-88-02472; CA No. A48266.


*  Appeal from judgment of the Circuit Court of Lane County, Honorable James R. Hargreaves, Judge. 91 Or App 1, 756 P2d 643 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the trial court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that denied the city's request to compel defendant, county election officials, to place on the state primary election ballot an "advisory question" that it wished to submit to city voters despite defendant, secretary of state's, order not to include the question.


OVERVIEW: The city sought to compel the officials to place on the state primary election ballot an "advisory question" that it wished to submit to city voters. The secretary had directed the officials not to place the advisory question on the ballot. The circuit court ordered defendants to include the measure on the ballot and the court of appeals ordered the circuit court reversed. The court affirmed the court of appeals, ruling that the question, which asked voters to choose one of two "nuclear-free zone" ordinances, was not a "measure" because it did not allow the voters to reject one or both of the ordinances. The question was not otherwise entitled to be placed on the ballot under any election law. Once the secretary determined that the question was without statutory authority, it was her duty, as the officer responsible for interpreting and enforcing the election laws, to direct the officials to refuse the ballot question. The officials' duties to conduct elections did not require them to include the city's non-measure on the election. Home rule was not implicated by county and state officials' refusal to do an act that was not required or contemplated by state law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the city's request to compel the officials to place on the state primary election ballot an "advisory question" that it wished to submit to city voters despite the secretary's order not to include the question.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state board of higher education and a representative affected class member, appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed a grant of partial summary judgment in favor of plaintiff taxpayers on all issues except a claim for monetary relief in a suit filed by the taxpayers to declare an executive veto invalid pursuant to Or. Const. art. V, § 15(a) (1921).


OVERVIEW: The state board of education and representative affected class member appealed a judgment affirming the grant of partial summary judgment in favor of the taxpayers in a suit which challenged the validity of a gubernatorial veto of an non-appropriations bill provision excluding certain types of college teacher earnings from the definition of salary. The court observed that the legislature had treated the veto as ineffective while the executive branch continued to make retirement contributions on the earnings that the bill sought to exclude. Younger v. Portland


SC No. S34287


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


305 Ore. 346; 752 P.2d 262; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 20


January 19, 1988, Argued and submitted  
March 29, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * LUBA No. 86-046; CA No. A43194.


*  Judicial review of final order of Land Use Board of Appeals. 86 Or App 211, 739 P2d 50 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals and of the Land Use Board of Appeals are reversed. The case is remanded to the Land Use Board of Appeals for reconsideration in light of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, residents of areas adjacent to a proposed development, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed the decision of the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA), made pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.825(1), affirming respondent city's approval for the proposed development.


OVERVIEW: Residents of areas adjacent to a proposed development challenged the city's approval of the development. The decision was affirmed by LUBA, which had exclusive jurisdiction to review the land use decisions of local governments under § 197.825(1). LUBA affirmed the city's decision and the Court of Appeals affirmed LUBA's decision. The court reversed and remanded to LUBA for reconsideration. The court stated that LUBA could have reversed or remanded the local government's land use decision if the decision was not supported by substantial evidence in the whole record, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.835(8)(a)(C). The issue was whether LUBA evaluated the substantiality of evidence supporting the decision by considering the supporting evidence alone or by considering all the evidence in the record, including countervailing evidence. The court of appeal held that LUBA's evaluation of substantiality properly considered only supporting evidence. However, LUBA was required to evaluate substantiality on the basis of the entire record and it was unclear whether or not this had been done.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the affirmation of LUBA's affirmance of the city's land use decision and remanded to LUBA for reconsideration in light of the entire record, not just supporting evidence.

 

193 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Libertarian Party of Oregon v. Roberts


SC No. S34180


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


305 Ore. 238; 750 P.2d 1147; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 32


October 8, 1987, Argued and submitted  
March 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 151821; CA No. A40378.


*  Appeal from Judgment of Marion County Circuit Court, Robert B. McConville, Judge. 85 Or App 450, 737 P2d 137 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals and the judgment of the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the Libertarian Party of Oregon, argued that requiring political organizations to demonstrate a certain level of support to qualify as a minor political party violated several provisions of the Oregon and United States Constitutions. The circuit court held that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 248.008 and 249.732 were constitutional and denied the declaratory and injunctive relief requested. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed. The Party appealed.


OVERVIEW: Within Oregon's statutory scheme for electing individuals to partisan public offices, there were five routes by which a partisan elective office could be sought: as a write-in candidate or as the nominee of a "major political party," "minor political party," "assembly of electors," or individual electors. The Party had been unable to demonstrate sufficient electoral support to regain statewide minor-party status. The Party argued that §§ 248.008 and 249.732 were unconstitutional because Oregon had not proved that the level of support required was necessary to further any legitimate state interest. The court found that because the requirements for minor-party recognition had remained relatively unchanged, the limited success of political organizations in achieving minor-party status in recent decades was little, if any, evidence that the purpose of §§ 248.008 and 249.732 was to protect the major political parties from nascent political organizations. Without identifying a statutory reason for the discrepancy, the court was left with the conclusion that minor-party and independent candidates had been less successful in Oregon because they had had less popular support in Oregon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

 

194 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wagner


SC No. S32635


Supreme Court of Oregon


305 Ore. 115; 752 P.2d 1136; 1988 Ore. LEXIS 8


September 1, 1987, Argued and submitted  
February 26, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On automatic and direct review of the judgment of conviction and sentence of death imposed by the Circuit Court for Linn County. William O. Lewis, Judge. TC No. 85061212.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and sentence of death by the trial court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court for Linn County (Oregon), which convicted defendant, upon his guilty plea, of aggravated murder in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095, and which imposed upon defendant a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought to represent himself in his trial for aggravated murder. The trial court, having determined that defendant had ample opportunity to consult with the individuals with whom he wished to consult, accepted defendant's plea of guilty to aggravated murder. A form of verdict required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.150(2) was submitted to the jury for sentencing, and the jury determined that the sentence of death was appropriate. Defendant appealed his judgment of conviction and the imposition of the death sentence. The court affirmed, finding that the Oregon constitution and the statutory law that provided for the possible penalty of death carefully defined aggravated murders. The constitution and statutes did not mandate death for such murderers. The statutes did not offend the Oregon constitution; rather, they provided the operative vehicle by which the purpose of Or. Const. art. I, § 40, could be attained. The Oregon scheme for sometimes imposing the death penalty did not offend the U.S. Constitution. Although his defense requesting life imprisonment was ultimately unsuccessful, defendant had a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of aggravated murder and sentenced him to death.

 

195 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Complaint of Griffith


SC No. S33097


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 575; 748 P.2d 86; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 2096


July 8, 1987, Argued and submitted  
December 30, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the decision of the Trial Panel of the Disciplinary Board of the Oregon State Bar under Rule 10.1. OSB No. 85-61.



DISPOSITION: Accused is disbarred from the practice of law in Oregon. The Oregon State Bar shall have judgment against him for its actual and necessary costs and disbursements incurred herein. ORS 9.536(4).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Trial Panel of the Disciplinary Board of the Oregon State Bar (Trial Panel) found that the accused attorney violated rules of conduct for attorneys and recommended that he be permanently disbarred from the practice of law. The decision was brought to the court for review pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.536.


OVERVIEW: The attorney and several partners were involved in several business ventures. The attorney participated in a sham sale in which he purportedly bought shares in a mortgage company from one of his partners in order that the partner avoid reporting his ownership in the mortgage company in the annual report of a bank for which he was an officer. The attorney also was involved in several transactions involving legal clients who secured financing from the mortgage company. The Oregon State Bar proved by clear and convincing evidence that the attorney's involvement in the sham stock sale constituted a violation of disciplinary rules because the attorney entered into the transaction knowing that it was designed to circumvent state and federal law. The attorney violated the disciplinary rule prohibiting dishonest and fraudulent conduct because, in conduct with a business partner, he knowingly and unlawfully acquired over 25 percent of the voting power in a bank without prior approval. Though the attorney was not personally involved in the representation of clients whose legal business concerned the companies in which he had an interest, he was responsible for notifying them of the conflict.


OUTCOME: The attorney was found guilty of four causes involving dishonesty and six causes involving conflicts of interest and was disbarred from the practice of law.

 

196 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lissy


SC No. S34184


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 455; 747 P.2d 345; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 2085


October 7, 1987, Argued and submitted  
December 15, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 10-84-08136; CA No. A35041.


*  Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court, William A. Beckett, Judge. 85 Or App 484, 737 P2d 617 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the Court of Appeals and the circuit court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of aggravated murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by the trial court of aggravated murder. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of the trial court and appellate court. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress certain recorded telephone conversations between himself and others and the recorded out-of-court statements of a co-conspirator. On appeal, the court held that state wiretap statutes, Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.724 and Or. Rev. Stat. § 165.540(1)(a), were inapplicable to a conversation between a police officer and a defendant. The court also found that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress a conversation between defendant and a third party. The court stated that as to conversations between a defendant and a third party, the consent of only one of the parties was required for a wiretap to be legal. The record showed that the State had, in fact, obtained the consent of the third party before recording his conversation with defendant. The court noted that the state wiretap statutes were not less restrictive than federally imposed standards.


OUTCOME: The decisions of the appellate court and the trial court were affirmed.

 

197 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Okeke


SC No. S33660


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 367; 745 P.2d 418; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1969


May 12, 1987, Argued and submitted  
November 17, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC DA No. 280069; CA No. A35902.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County District Court, Linda L. Bergman, Judge. 82 Or App 393, 728 P2d 872 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The judgment of the trial court is reversed, and the case is remanded to that court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a concealed weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a passenger in a car stopped for a traffic violation. A deputy sheriff detected the odor of alcohol on defendant's breath. When defendant argued with the officer and repeatedly demanded to know when the driver and passengers were to leave, the officer took her into custody and delivered her to a center for alcoholism intervention, which was a facility operated by a nonprofit corporation under contract with the county. During the intake procedure, the center's night supervisor searched defendant's purse and found a small pistol. Defendant was convicted of possession of a concealed weapon. The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction, holding that constitutional restraints applied to the search of her purse at the center. The State appealed, and the court found that although defendant was seized within the meaning of the constitutional provisions governing searches and seizures, she was not arrested on suspicion of any crime. The court held that the evidence found while searching her purse was not to be used to convict her where she was taken involuntarily by the police to the center.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision reversing defendant's conviction for possession of a concealed weapon and remanded to the district court.

 

198 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tanner


SC No. S33523


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 312; 745 P.2d 757; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1972


March 4, 1987, Argued and submitted  
November 17, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 84-0973; CA No. A36777.


* Appeal from Judgment of Washington County Circuit Court, Alan C. Bonebrake, Judge. 82 Or App 296, 728 P2d 47 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed; the suppression order of the circuit court is reinstated. Remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was indicted for theft, challenged a ruling of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which, on appeal by the state, reversed the trial court's decision to grant defendant's motion to suppress evidence uncovered during the illegal search of the residence of a third party as unconstitutional under Or. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: Defendant took video tapes and equipment from his employer's business and pledged them to a third party as collateral for a loan. Police officers, acting under an invalid warrant, discovered these items during a search of the third party's residence. The discovery led to defendant's indictment for theft. He filed a motion to suppress the evidence seized from the third party's residence, arguing that the officers' actions violated Or. Const. art. 1, § 9. The trial court granted defendant's motion. The state appealed; the court of appeals reversed. On appeal, the court reversed and reinstated the trial court's order granting the motion to suppress. The court held that defendant's entrustment of the stolen property to the third party was sufficient to establish a right under § 9 against the unlawful search that uncovered the effects. In so holding, the court held that § 9 interests were not limited to possessory interests in places or things and that such interests included the right to privacy. The court found that an analysis of § 9 was different than an analysis under the Fourth Amendment, which dealt with "expectations" of privacy rather than rights of privacy.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, the suppression order of the trial court was reinstated, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

199 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Molodyh v. Truck Ins. Exchange


SC No. S32789


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 290; 744 P.2d 992; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1859


September 4, 1986, Argued and submitted  
November 3, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * Argued and submitted September 4, 1986. TC No. 149,522; CA No. A33853.


*  On appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County, Honorable Rodney W. Miller, Judge. 77 Or App 619, 714 P2d 257 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed, but upon different grounds. The trial court is reversed. The case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured filed an action seeking to have a jury determine the amount of loss to his insured property. The appellate court (Oregon) reversed and remanded the trial court's decision to grant summary judgment in favor of defendant insurance company. The insurance company challenged the appellate court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The insured's property, which was covered by the insurance company under a multi-risk policy, was damaged by fire. Thereafter, the insured submitted a proof of loss, however the insurance company estimated that the property loss was less than that claimed by the insured. Subsequently, the insured demanded an appraisal. After the appraisal, the insurance company awarded the amount determined by the appraiser. The appellate court, reversing the trial court's decision, held that the mandatory nature of the appraisal provision deprived the insured of his state constitutional right to a jury trial. The issue in the present appeal, was whether Or. Rev. Stat. § 743.648, which set forth an appraisal procedure violated Or. Const. art. I, § 17. First, the court held that the insured in demanding arbitration, was merely following the condition precedent of the appraisal requirement. Moreover, the court determined that the insured continued to assert his right to a jury trial throughout the litigation. Consequently, the court held that the insured was entitled to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision reversing the trial court's decision to enter summary judgment in favor of the insurance company.

 

200 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Adult & Family Services Div. v. Tuttle


SC No. S34077


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 270; 744 P.2d 990; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1856


September 1, 1987, Argued and submitted  
October 27, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. D8507-65133; CA No. A40369.


*  On appeal from the Circuit Court of Multnomah County. Katherine B. Nachtigal, Judge. 85 Or App 221, 735 P2d 1304 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the trial court is reversed; the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant alleged father filed a petition for review challenging a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a trial court's order granting the alleged father's motion to dismiss. Petitioner State or Oregon filed an action to establish the alleged father's paternity.


OVERVIEW: The State filed a petition to establish the alleged father's paternity. The alleged father argued that the action was barred under Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.135(3) and filed a motion to dismiss the State's petition. Upon review, the trial court granted the alleged father's motion. The alleged father filed a petition for review that challenged a subsequent appellate court judgment reversing the trial court's order. Upon further review, the appellate court's judgment was affirmed by the court. The court held that the ten-year statute of limitations on paternity actions under Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.135(3) violated Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The court concluded that Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.135(3) was unconstitutional because illegitimate children were entitled to seek support from their fathers. The court noted that the statute of limitations under § 109.135(3) placed an undue burden on the ascertainment of paternity that neither advanced the accuracy of the outcome of the case nor diminished the likelihood of fraudulent claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.
